The team, which the bank is continuing to invest in, has 12 specialists in the art market across credit, wealth planning and philanthropy. The bank’s clients that already have loans keep them, while utilization has remained around 70% this year, according to Watson.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/2024/08/16/rich-art-market-collateral-cash-loan-debt/\n\nSource 7:\nWhen the cultural anthropologists got to work on him, his final link to the old moral absolutes was severed. From their comparisons of primitive cultures, and, later, our own, many anthropologists came to the view that the ethics of a people are relative. By this they do not mean that ethics are unimportant, but rather that they are not to be judged by any abstract conceptions of "right" or "wrong." For if we realize that other cultures and ethics are "equally valid," to use Ruth Benedict\'s phrase, then we will be jogged into giving up all the more readily our outworn traditions and our illusions of individual autonomy. "It is not any particular set of values," another anthropologist explains, "but a way of looking at them that is important."\n\nA half-century has gone by and the relativistic, social view of man idea is still gaining. The appetite for cultural anthropology, for example, has been growing at such a rate that Ruth Benedict\'s Patterns of Culture, first published in 1931, has reached, after a phenomenal newsstand sale, the No. 1 best-seller spot in the Mentor paper-book series.\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/article/groupthink-fortune-1952/\n\nSource 8:\nThis is compelling for two reasons, she says: One, the individualization of American society has changed the way we engage in our communities, with fewer places to anchor our sense of identity to; two; we’re spending a lot more time in our jobs.\n\n“People are finding that sense of self in their work because that\'s a kind of ready made place for us culturally,” she explains. “And if you\'re working so many hours, it can be a way of feeling some kind of sense of purpose or identity to have a job that aligns with that.”\n\nPurpose is what differentiates humans from other animals, she and Martela both point out. He says that humans can ask “why” questions—Why am I doing this task? Why am I doing this job? We are thus seeking justification or value in our activities, he says, which leads us to meaning and purpose: “We want our lives to matter and make a difference, to have value.”\n\nFewer mundane tasks, more human connections\n\n\nurl: https://fortune.com/well/article/a-i-help-find-purpose-at-work/\n\nSource 9:\nThe booming U.S. art market is recording annual sales estimated at $5 billion to $6 billion and growing approximately 20 percent a year. According to Katherine Fleming, president and CEO of the J. Paul Getty Trust, creating and consuming art is tied to wellness, as it "makes us happy" and "makes it possible for us to express emotion" [1]. Art also has the ability to reduce stress and anxiety, with research showing that looking at art can lower blood pressure and give us an endorphin rush [1]. This emotional resonance is a key factor in art's impact on culture, as it allows us to tap into our shared human experiences and connect with others on a deeper level.

Art also plays a significant role in shaping our cultural identity and values. As Noah Horowitz, CEO of Art Basel, notes, art has the power to "create inspiring moments of connectivity and ultimately transform cities" [1]. This is evident in the way that art can bring people together, foster a sense of community, and provide a platform for social commentary and critique. Furthermore, art can also influence our cultural values by challenging our assumptions and pushing us to think differently about the world around us. As Ruth Benedict, a cultural anthropologist, notes, "it is not any particular set of values, but a way of looking at them that is important" [7]. This highlights the importance of art in shaping our cultural values and encouraging us to think critically about the world around us.

As Noah Horowitz, CEO of Art Basel, notes, art has the power to "create inspiring moments of connectivity and ultimately transform cities" [1]. This is evident in the way that art can bring people together, foster a sense of community, and provide a platform for social commentary and critique. Furthermore, art can also influence our cultural values by challenging our assumptions and pushing us to think differently about the world around us. As Ruth Benedict, a cultural anthropologist, notes, "it is not any particular set of values, but a way of looking at them that is important" [7]. This highlights the importance of art in shaping our cultural values and encouraging us to think critically about the world around us.

The impact of art on culture is also closely tied to its economic and social benefits. The global art market is a significant contributor to many economies, with annual sales estimated at $5 billion to $6 billion and growing approximately 20 percent a year [9]. Paul Getty Trust, creating and consuming art is tied to wellness, as it "makes us happy" and "makes it possible for us to express emotion" [1]. Art also has the ability to reduce stress and anxiety, with research showing that looking at art can lower blood pressure and give us an endorphin rush [1]. This emotional resonance is a key factor in art's impact on culture, as it allows us to tap into our shared human experiences and connect with others on a deeper level.

Art also plays a significant role in shaping our cultural identity and values. As Noah Horowitz, CEO of Art Basel, notes, art has the power to "create inspiring moments of connectivity and ultimately transform cities" [1]. This is evident in the way that art can bring people together, foster a sense of community, and provide a platform for social commentary and critique. Furthermore, art can also influence our cultural values by challenging our assumptions and pushing us to think differently about the world around us. As Ruth Benedict, a cultural anthropologist, notes, "it is not any particular set of values, but a way of looking at them that is important" [7]. This highlights the importance of art in shaping our cultural values and encouraging us to think critically about the world around us.

The impact of art on culture is also closely tied to its economic and social benefits. The global art market is a significant contributor to many economies, with annual sales estimated at $5 billion to $6 billion and growing approximately 20 percent a year [9]. Additionally, art can also have a positive impact on our mental and physical health, with research showing that engaging with art can improve our cognitive function and overall well-being [5]. Furthermore, art can also influence our cultural values by challenging our assumptions and pushing us to think differently about the world around us. This highlights the importance of art in shaping our cultural values and encouraging us to think critically about the world around us.

The impact of art on culture is also closely tied to its economic and social benefits. Furthermore, art can also influence our cultural values by challenging our assumptions and pushing us to think differently about the world around us. As Ruth Benedict, a cultural anthropologist, notes, "it is not any particular set of values, but a way of looking at them that is important" [7]. This highlights the importance of art in shaping our cultural values and encouraging us to think critically about the world around us.

The impact of art on culture is also closely tied to its economic and social benefits. The global art market is a significant contributor to many economies, with annual sales estimated at $5 billion to $6 billion and growing approximately 20 percent a year [9]. Additionally, art can also have a positive impact on our mental and physical health, with research showing that engaging with art can improve our cognitive function and overall well-being [5]. As Maddy Dychtwald, author of Ageless Aging, notes, "we have agency that we never thought we had before" [5], highlighting the ways in which art can empower us to take control of our lives and improve our overall health and well-being.
